Contents as variant A\n> except: \\[381–392\\] blank.\n\n*Jacket:* As 233.1a. (*Spring 1933*).\n\n> Front flap:
\n> The sinister and depraved figure of Popeye dominates as extraordinary\n> a novel as our contemporary national literature can boast. *Sanctuary*\n> is a story-teller’s *tour de force*, a tense and impassioned narration\n> of a horrific aspect of life that only the vigorous and the\n> healthy–minded can bear without wincing. It is a virile, challenging\n> novel that cannot be ignored or set aside. *Sanctuary* arouses the\n> most extreme enthusiasm or revulsion; it holds spellbound its most\n> ardent admirers and bitterest foes from first sentence to last. (*Fall\n> 1933*)\n\n#### 233.1c. \n> This celebrated book, the sixth of William Faulkner’s novels in order\n> of publication, first appeared on February 9, 1931, but a draft of the\n> manuscript had been finished nearly two years earlier. When Mr.\n> Faulkner saw the galley proofs of that version of *Sanctuary*, he felt\n> “that there were but two things to do: tear it up or rewrite it.”\n>\n> He chose the latter course and did a thorough revision of the book,\n> cutting some sections entirely and adding new ones, rewriting other\n> sections in whole or in part, and rearranging the order of the\n> narration. He thus succeeded in making it a book which “would not\n> shame . . . too much,” as he put it, *The Sound and the Fury* and *As\n> I Lay Dying*, which had been published in the two preceding years.\n> (*Inside of jacket blank; back flap lists Faulkner titles published in\n> the ML through fall 1964.*)\n\nThe letterpress plate for the 233.1c title page appears to have been\nused for 233.1d with the line for Faulkner’s introduction and the rule\nat the foot of the page removed and torchbearer H substituted for\ntorchbearer D3.\n\nThe memorandum issuing the instruction to drop the introductions from\n*Sanctuary* and *Absalom, Absalom!* was dated 14 November 1962 (Box 538,\nML spring 1962 folder). The introduction to Faulkner’s *Light in August*\nwas also dropped around this time. The earliest printing of *Sanctuary*\nwithout the introduction was in 1963 or 1964. Introductions appear to\nhave been dropped when Faulkner titles in the ML began to appear in\nuniform non-pictorial jackets on coated white paper. The earliest use of\nthe uniform Faulkner jacket was for *Light in August* in spring 1963. By\n1967 the regular ML included ten Faulkner titles in white uniform\njackets. Four, including *Sanctuary*, were existing ML titles that were\noutfitted in new jackets. Four titles—*Selected Short Stories*,\n*Intruder in the Dust*, *A Fable*, and *Pylon*—were new to the series.\n*The Sound and the Fury* and *As I Lay Dying*, had previously been\ncombined in a single ML volume."

\n> Implacable fate plays a major role in the novels of Thomas Hardy. In\n> *Tess of the D’Urbervilles* it wreaks havoc in the life of its heroine\n> and makes her the victim of forces beyond her control. Her story, grim\n> and unrelieved in its pathos, is told in the manner of heroic tragedy.\n> The fate of the novel itself is, in a sense, a measure of its quality\n> and the strong feelings it aroused. Weber accepted but added:\n\n> I assume that you will take care to get the correct definitive TEXT of\n> this novel, and will avoid using the far-from-definitive edition first\n> issued by the Modern Library. . . . NO EDITION OF TESS PUBLISHED\n> BEFORE 1912 will provide you with a satisfactory text, and many an\n> edition issued since 1912 still perpetuates the omissions of earlier\n> editions. Macmillan’s London edition of 1912, and Harper’s\n> “Anniversary Edition” issued in New York in 1920–21, or more recent\n> reprints of either of these, are the only satisfactory sources of text\n> for your projected edition. If you have any doubt about what’s what,\n> please give me a chance to examine and comment on whatever you are\n> going to use. I shall have occasion, in my introduction, to speak of\n> Hardy’s revisions, and you will of course want your edition to be a\n> definitive job (Weber to Stein, 3 July 1950).\n\nStein checked with Harper’s about using the plates of their 1920\nedition. KLOPFER | THE MODERN LIBRARY | NEW YORK\n\nPp. \\[i–iv\\] v–xi \\[xii\\], 1–678 \\[679–684\\]. \\[1–21\\]16\n\\[22\\]12\n\n\\[i\\] half title; \\[ii\\] pub. note D12; \\[iii\\] title; \\[iv\\] *First\nModern Library Edition* | 1932 | \\[short double rule\\]; v–xi CONTENTS;\n\\[xii\\] blank; 1–678 text; \\[679–683\\] ML list; \\[684\\] blank. (*Spring\n1932*)\n\n*Contents:* Evangeline – The Courtship of Miles Standish – The Song of\nHiawatha – Tales of a Wayside Inn – Voices of the Night – Ballads and\nOther Poems – The Spanish Student – The Belfry of Bruges, and Other\nPoems – The Seaside and the Fireside – Miscellaneous.\n\n*Jacket:* Uniform typographic jacket D. (*Spring 1932*)\n\n> Front flap:
\n> Generation after generation of Americans has been brought up on the\n> poems of Henry Wadsworth Longfellow. To him can be attributed a major\n> share of what love for poetry great masses of our people cherish. Such\n> poems as “The Song of Hiawatha,” “Evangeline,” “The Courtship of Miles\n> Standish,” “Tales of a Wayside Inn,” and many others are part of our\n> heritage, and we read them over and over again with a nostalgia for\n> our youth and a new appreciation of Longfellow’s place among the great\n> lyricists of our national literature. (*Spring 1938*)\n\nOriginal ML collection. Published April 1932. *WR* 7 May 1932. First\nprinting: Not ascertained.\n\nLongfellow was the most commercially successful American poet of his\ntime. There were only three years between 1845 and 1851 when\nLongfellow’s income from poetry did not exceed—sometimes\nsubstantially—his salary as the Smith Professor of Modern Languages at\nHarvard. He resigned from Harvard in 1854, and from 1875 until his death\nhe received $4,000 a year from his older books alone—more than twice\nwhat he had earned as a professor (Charvat, p. 113). His poetry remained\nsufficiently lucrative in the twentieth century for Houghton Mifflin\nCo., the ultimate successor of Longfellow’s original Boston publisher,\nTicknor and Fields, to be concerned when the Modern Library announced\nits edition of Longfellow. Cerf received a letter from Robert Linscott\nat Houghton Mifflin:\n\n> A slight tremor of agitation swept over this office yesterday when we\n> first noticed your announcement . . . \\[Longfellow is the\\] Poet of\n> Poets most closely associated with Houghton Mifflin Company. Suckled\n> at our even then venerable bosom, he was for years our mainstay, and\n> office boys learned to lisp the leisured cadences of “Evangeline.”\n\nLinscott indicated that he presumed the ML would be using only works for\nwhich the copyright had expired, and he asked whether any courtesy\nacknowledgment would be made (Linscott to Cerf, 15 January 1932).\n\nThe ML initially intended to base its edition on *The Poems of Henry W.\nLongfellow*, published in 1901 by the A. L. Burt Co. in its Home Library\nseries. Linscott wrote again four days later:\n\n> You’re a trusting soul to take the Burt Home Library “Longfellow”\n> which contains only the poems which apparently were out of copyright\n> before 1900. In other words, out of seven and a half pages of contents\n> as given in the Complete Cambridge Edition, only approximately two and\n> a half are included in the Burt Longfellow (Linscott to Cerf, 19\n> January 1932).\n\nLinscott promised to send a copy of the Houghton Mifflin’s Complete\nCambridge Edition with the copyrighted material marked.\n\nAt this point Ferris Greenslet, the general manager of the Houghton\nMifflin trade department, whom Tebbel has described as “the dominant\nfigure at Houghton Mifflin in the thirties” (vol. 3, p. 537),\nintervened. He wrote Cerf:\n\n> I am rather embarrassed to know just what to say about it \\[the\n> Longfellow matter\\]. As we still have a fair amount of the Longfellow\n> material in copyright and therefore the only complete editions of that\n> poet’s writings, we have always been rather tender on the topic and\n> have asked our publishing colleagues with whom we have reciprocal\n> relations to lay off Longfellow, and where they have done so, have\n> tried to give them preferential treatment in copyright matters.\n>\n> Are you really hell-bent to put Longfellow now into the Modern Library\n> cheek-for-jowl with Oscar Wilde, Anatole France, *et cetera*, or can\n> you wait until towards the end of the present bad decade when I\n> believe the last copyright expires? (Greenslet to Cerf, 1 February\n> 1932)\n\nBoth Cerf and Klopfer appealed to Linscott to send the list of\nLongfellow material that had gone out of copyright since 1901 (Cerf to\nLinscott, 29 January 1932; Klopfer to Linscott, 30 January 1932).\nLinscott responded, “The Longfellow problem is a little murky as you may\nhave gathered from Mr. Greenslet’s letter. . . . Under the\ncircumstances, I’m afraid I shan’t be able to help you out—at least at\npresent” (Linscott to Klopfer, 2 February 1932).\n\nIn the end the ML appears to have secured reliable information about the\ncopyright status of Longfellow’s poems. The ML was able to include\nnearly all of Longfellow’s best-known works.\n\nWhen the ML Longfellow was published Cerf sent a copy to Greenslet, who\nacknowledged, “It is, of course, innocent of any copyright evil. Best\nwishes for its success!” (Greenslet to Cerf, 28 April 1932). The ML\nedition of Longfellow remained in print through the 1970s. There was a\nnew typesetting in 1944, but no attempt was made to add material that\nhad entered the public domain since 1932.\n\n*The Poems of Longfellow* and *The Autobiography of Benjamin Franklin*\n(236) were the last new ML titles to appear in uniform typographic\njacket D. Most new ML titles were being published in pictorial jackets\nby 1932, and Cerf reacted angrily when he saw the Longfellow and\nFranklin jackets. The Franklin jacket was replaced almost immediately by\na pictorial jacket. The uniform typographic jacket for *The Poems of\nLongfellow* remained in use until the early 1940s.\n\nCerf complained about poor sales of the Longfellow volume in the 1930s,\nbut it appears to have increased in popularity in the 1940s. *The Poems\nof Longfellow* sold 8,504 copies during the eighteen-month period May\n1942–October 1943, placing it low in the first quarter of ML and Giant\ntitles. Many of these sales were probably to people serving in the armed\nservices. Sales declined to 4,528 copies during the twelve-month period\nNovember 1951–October 1952, but that was still enough for it to hold on\nto its first-quarter ranking.\n\n#### 235.1b. (*Spring 1932*)\n\n*Contents:* Franklin’s Draft Scheme of the Autobiography – The\nAutobiography – The Dogood Papers – Preface to Poor Richard, 1733 – Poor\nRichard’s Almanack, 1749 (How to Get Riches) – *From* Poor Richard’s\nAlmanack, 1756 – *From* Poor Richard’s Almanack, 1757 – The Way to\nWealth: Preface to Poor Richard Improved, 1758 – On the Price of Corn,\nand Management of the Poor – Advice to a Young Tradesman – Journal of a\nVoyage from London to Philadelphia.\n\n*Jacket A:* Uniform typographic jacket D. (*Spring 1932*)\n\n*Jacket B:* Pictorial in moderate blue (182) and yellowish gray (93) on\ncoated white paper depicting Franklin taking notes while conducting an\nexperiment; borders and lettering in moderate blue, background in\nyellowish gray. (*Spring 1932*)\n\n> Front flap:
\n> The wide range of Benjamin Franklin’s interests and activities opened\n> the doors of the world to him. Printer, inventor, philosopher,\n> diplomat, champion of liberty, he made his influence felt not only\n> upon his own time, but upon every American generation. His wise maxims\n> and shrewd observations have become national axioms. His\n> *Autobiography*, as rich in material as it is engaging in style, is\n> one of those rare documents that reveals both a rich personality and\n> the crucial events that transpired during his lifetime. (*Spring\n> 1938*)\n\nOriginal ML collection. Published April 1932. *WR* 7 May 1932. First\nprinting: Not ascertained. Discontinued 1971/72.\n\nCerf originally planned to publish Franklin’s *Autobiography* by itself.\nThree months before publication he decided to add more of Franklin’s\nwritings so the book would be a better value. Goodman, who suggested the\nadditional contents, received $100 for his work on the volume.\n\n*The Autobiography of Benjamin Franklin* and *The Poems of Longfellow*\n(235) were the last new ML titles to appear in uniform typographic\njacket D. Most new ML titles were being published in pictorial jackets\nby 1932, and Cerf reacted angrily when he saw the Franklin and\nLongfellow jackets. He wrote the ML’s printer, “Please note that from\nthis day forth, I don’t want any Modern Library jacket to go to press\nunless it has been personally O.K.’d by me. The O.K. must be for both\nthe typography of the jacket, and the stock used for the jacket. I think\nthat the jackets for Longfellow and Franklin are rotten” (Cerf to\nWilliam Simon, Parkway Printing, 22 April 1932).\n\nThe Franklin jacket was replaced almost immediately by a newly designed\npictorial jacket (jacket B, described above). Copies of the first\nprinting are found in both jackets. The pictorial jacket, which remained\nin use through the end of the 1930s, was one of the least attractive\njackets the ML ever used.\n\n*The Autobiography of Benjamin Franklin* sold 5,569 copies during the\neighteen-month period May 1942–October 1943, placing it low in the\nsecond quarter of ML and Giant titles. It sold 3,468 copies during the\ntwelve-month period November 1951–October 1952—a decline in real terms\ncompared with the Second World War, when book sales of all kinds were\nbooming, but enough to place it high in the second quarter of ML and\nGiant titles.\n\n#### 236.1b. Signed:\nL. (*Spring 1932*)\n\n> Front flap:
\n> The simplicity and clarity of Ernest Hemingway’s profoundly moving\n> romance, *A Farewell to Arms*, have won acclaim from the critics,\n> support from the public, and that final wreath – a host of imitators.\n> Critics have spared no adjectives in praise of his sure-footed,\n> athletic prose; readers have found enchantment in this poignant love\n> story, and writers generally have drawn sustenance from Hemingway’s\n> strength. *A Farewell to Arms* is an exciting book, true to its time\n> and true to the convictions and vigor of its author. (*Fall 1934*)\n\nOriginally published by Charles Scribner’s Sons, 1929. ML edition (pp.\n\\[v–vii\\], \\[1\\]–355) printed from Scribner plates with Scribner half\ntitle used as a fly title. Published May 1932. *WR* 28 May 1932. First\nprinting: 7,000 copies. Discontinued spring 1953.\n\nThe ML offered Scribner’s a $6,000 advance against royalties of 12 cents\na copy, the same terms as for Hemingway’s *Sun Also Rises* (190). Cerf\ninitially hoped to publish *A Farewell to Arms* in September 1931 (Cerf\nto Maxwell Perkins, Scribner’s, 4 December 1930). The ML edition may\nhave been delayed so that it would not conflict with the August 1931\npublication of Grosset & Dunlap’s one-dollar edition. Cerf cabled Ford\nin Paris to ask him to write the introduction, offering $75 and\nindicating that it was needed by 15 March (Cerf to Ford, 14 January\n1932).\n\nThere were printings in 1933 of 4,000 copies (January) and 3,000 copies\n(July), at least nine additional printings of 2,000 copies each by March\n1941, and four printings between June 1941 and December 1942 totaling\n14,000 copies. Sales during the first five years were as follows: 4,111\ncopies; 6,020 copies; 2,656 copies; 2,585 copies; and 2,632 copies (Cerf\nto Kenneth Roberts, 23 November 1938). *A Farewell to Arms* sold 12,287\ncopies during the eighteen-month period May 1942–October 1943, placing\nit sixteenth out of 271 ML and Giant titles and well ahead of *The Sun\nAlso Rises*. It sold 7,766 copies during the twelve-month period\nNovember 1951–October 1952, making it fourteenth out of the 100\nbest-selling titles in the regular ML.\n\nScribner’s decided in the early 1950s to promote its backlist more\nvigorously and terminated the ML’s reprint contracts for all three of\nthe Hemingway titles in the series (Whitney Darrow, Scribner’s, to Cerf,\n29 October 1952). A few months later the ML reported that *A Farewell to\nArms* was completely out of stock (ML to Charles Burgess, Jr.,\nScribner’s, 16 February 1953). Scribner’s published a $3.00 hardbound\nedition in March 1953 and a $1.65 paperback in 1962.\n\n#### 237b. (*Spring 1932*)\n\n> Front flap:
\n> The devotees of the novels of Joseph Conrad divide their enthusiasm\n> equally between *Victory* and *Lord Jim* (No. 186 in the Modern\n> Library). Written in 1914, just before the outbreak of the World War,\n> *Victory* has steadily grown in prestige through almost a quarter of a\n> century. New readers come upon it as a rare discovery and old readers\n> return to it for the sheer enjoyment of its prose style and subtle\n> characterization. *Victory* represents Joseph Conrad at the apex of\n> his story-telling powers. (*Fall 1936*)\n\nOriginally published in U.S. by Doubleday, Page & Co., 1915. ML edition\nprinted from plates made from a new Doubleday, Doran typesetting; the\nplates appear to have been used exclusively by the ML. Published June\n1932. *WR* 2 July 1932. First printing: Not ascertained. Discontinued\n1971/72.\n\nCerf tried on several occasions to secure reprint rights to *Victory*.\nIn 1928 he offered an advance of $3,600 against a 12-cent royalty—a\nroyalty that the ML was then paying for only one other title (Cerf to\nNelson Doubleday, 12 December 1928). Doubleday replied, “We do\nappreciate the high standing of the Modern Library, but we believe it is\nunwise to let the Conrad title go” (Nelson Doubleday to Cerf, 21\nDecember 1928). *Victory* was included in the Sun Dial Library published\nby Garden City Publishing Co., the Doubleday, Doran subsidiary that\nspecialized in hardcover reprints. The ML’s purchase of the Sun Dial\nLibrary in 1930 gave the ML reprint rights to any Doubleday title\nincluded in the series. *Victory* was the seventh of eleven Sun Dial\nLibrary titles that the ML added. The ML paid Doubleday, Doran a $1,500\nadvance against royalties of 10 cents a copy.\n\n*Victory* sold 7,335 copies during the eighteen-month period May\n1942–October 1943, placing it low in the first quarter of ML and Giant\ntitles. *Lord Jim* (210) sold 9,450 copies during the same period. Sales\nof *Victory* totaled 70,000 copies by 1949. By the early 1950s Conrad\nwas one of the ML’s best-selling authors. *Victory* sold 7,966 copies\nduring the twelve-month period November 1951–October 1952, making it the\ntwelfth best-selling title in the regular ML. *Lord Jim* was the eighth\nbest-selling title.\n\n#### 238b. Title page reset (c. 1940)\n\nVictory | by | JOSEPH CONRAD | \\[torchbearer D1 at right; 3-line imprint\nat left\\] THE | MODERN LIBRARY | NEW YORK | \\[rule\\]\n\nPagination and collation as 238a variant.\n\nContents as 238a except: \\[ii\\] blank; \\[iv\\] COPYRIGHT, 1915, 1921, BY\n| DOUBLEDAY, PAGE & COMPANY | COPYRIGHT, 1915, BY JOSEPH CONRAD;\n\\[387–391\\] ML list; \\[392–393\\] ML Giants list; \\[394–398\\] blank.\n(*Spring 1941*)\n\n*Jacket A:* Non-pictorial in deep reddish orange (36) and black on cream\npaper with title and author in black on inset cream panel; background in\ndeep reddish orange with series and torchbearer in reverse below inset\npanel. Front flap as 238a. (*Spring 1941*)\n\n> Front flap revised:
\n> Written in 1914, just before the outbreak of the First World War,\n> *Victory* was immediately acclaimed the work of a master. It has grown\n> steadily in the estimation of a new generation of readers who have\n> come upon it as a rare discovery. Older readers go back to it for the\n> sheer pleasure of its graceful prose and its subtleties of\n> characterization. *Victory* is one of the novels by which Joseph\n> Conrad achieved the fullest measure of his gifts as story-teller. It\n> vies for favor with *Lord Jim* (No. 186), *Nostromo* (No. 275) and\n> *Heart of Darkness* (in *Great Modern Short Stories*, No. 168).\n> (*Spring 1955*)\n\n*Jacket B:* Pictorial in moderate yellow (87), strong green (141),\nstrong bluish green (160) and black on coated white paper depicting palm\ntrees and two birds in flight against strong bluish green sea and\nmoderate yellow sky; lettering in reverse. First printing (1932)\n\n\\[within double rules\\] A HIGH WIND IN | JAMAICA | \\[within square\nbrackets\\] THE INNOCENT VOYAGE | \\[rule\\] | BY | RICHARD HUGHES |\n\\[rule\\] | INTRODUCTION BY | ISABEL PATERSON | \\[rule\\] | \\[torchbearer\nA2\\] | \\[rule\\] | BENNETT A. CERF · DONALD S. KLOPFER | THE MODERN\nLIBRARY | NEW YORK\n\nPp. \\[i–iv\\] v–xxii, ix \\[x\\], 1–136, \\[*2*\\], 137–234, \\[*2*\\],\n235–312, \\[*2*\\], 313–399 \\[400–402\\]. \\[1–13\\]16\n\\[14\\]8\n\n\\[i\\] half title; \\[ii\\] frontispiece; \\[iii\\] title; \\[iv\\] A HIGH WIND\nIN JAMAICA (THE INNOCENT VOYAGE) | *Copyright,* 1929, *by* RICHARD\nHUGHES | \\[short double rule\\] | *Introduction Copyright,* 1932, *by*\nTHE MODERN | LIBRARY, INC. | \\[short double rule\\] | *First Modern\nLibrary Edition* | 1932; v–xxii *Preface* signed p. xxii: Isabel Paterson. | New\nYork, *January*, 1932.; ix ILLUSTRATIONS; \\[x\\] blank; 1–399 text\nwith illustrations facing pp. 136, 234, 312; \\[400–402\\] blank.\n\n*Jacket:* Pictorial in strong green (141) and black on cream paper\ndepicting a schooner sailing into a lagoon; borders in strong green,\nlettering in black. Signed: L. (*Spring 1932*)\n\n> Front flap:
\n> A band of pirates captures a vessel bound for England that contains a\n> miscellaneous cargo—including a group of children who have been driven\n> from their home by a West Indian hurricane. The difficulties\n> encountered by the well-meaning buccaneers with these incredible\n> children provide the frame-work for one of the most unusual books in\n> the Modern Library series—a shocking, nerve-tingling story that the\n> reader will never forget. (*Spring 1937*)\n\nOriginally published in U.S. as *The Innocent Voyage* by Harper & Bros.,\n1929. ML edition (pp. 1–399) printed from Harper plates. Published July\n1932. *WR* 2 July 1932. First printing: 5,000 copies. Discontinued 1\nJanuary 1958.\n\nThe ML paid Harper’s royalties of 10 cents a copy. Cerf had indicated\ninterest in a ML edition the year the novel was published. Noting his\npersonal enthusiasm for the book, he declared: “I think that I myself\nhave bought over 50 copies of it” (Cerf to Henry Hoyns, Harper & Bros.,\n5 August 1929). Hoyns assured him that Harper’s would be glad to talk\nabout a ML edition at the proper time (Hoyns to Cerf, 10 September\n1929). Two years later, when Cerf sent Harper’s the reprint contract, he\nstated: “I don’t believe that this book will break any sales records in\nthe Modern Library, but I am glad to have it for its prestige value and\nI have hopes that we may be able to enhance the popularity of the\nauthor’s other books” (Cerf to Hoyns, 5 December 1931). When the reprint\ncontract was renewed in 1937, Klopfer commented, “Bennett and I have a\nwarm spot in our hearts for this book and we want to keep it in the\nModern Library, and will do a special promotion job when the movie is\nreleased” (Klopfer to Hoyns, 1 February 1937).\n\nWhen the reprint contract was up for renewal in 1942, Hoyns informed\nKlopfer that Harper’s were about to about to sign a contract with the\nReaders Club for a reprint edition and that George Macy, the club’s\npresident, wanted the ML edition withdrawn while his edition was\navailable (Hoyns to Klopfer, 3 March 1942). Klopfer spoke with Macy and\nconvinced him that the ML edition would not interfere with the mail\norder sales of the Readers Club. He then told Hoyns that the ML would\nlike to renew the contract for another five years, giving Harper’s the\noption to cancel if Macy insisted on exclusive reprint rights (Klopfer\nto Hoyns, 5 March 1942). He later noted that a small unearned royalty\nremained from the $750 advance the ML paid when the contract was renewed\nin 1937. He suggested keeping the book in the ML at the same royalty but\nwithout an advance; if Harper’s wanted an advance, he indicated that the\nML could pay $250 but didn’t want to go higher in case the Readers Club\nedition hurt sales (Klopfer to Hoyns, 10 April 1942).\n\nIn 1956 the College Department of Harper & Bros. expressed interest in\nadding *A High Wind in Jamaica* and Richard Wright’s *Native Son* (349)\nto the Harper Modern Classics series. William H. Rose, Jr., the Harper\ndirector of sales and promotion, asked Klopfer about their status in the\nML, noting that Harper Modern Classics carried a short discount and had\ntextbook distribution only (Rose to Klopfer, 20 July 1956). Klopfer\nreplied that neither title was strong enough to withstand competition\nfrom an inexpensive Harper edition. “As long as your college department\nwants NATIVE SON and HIGH WIND IN JAMAICA, we’ll let them run out of the\nModern Library as soon as our present stocks are exhausted” (Klopfer to\nRose, 25 July 1956).\n\n*A High Wind in Jamaica* sold 4,551 copies during the eighteen-month\nperiod May 1942–October 1943, placing it in the third quarter of ML and\nGiant titles. It did not rank among the one-hundred best-selling titles\nin the regular ML during the twelve-month period November 1951–October\n1952.\n\n#### 239b. Title page reset (c. 1940)\n\nA | HIGH WIND | IN JAMAICA | (THE INNOCENT VOYAGE) | by RICHARD HUGHES |\n*Introduction by* ISABEL PATERSON | \\[torchbearer D4 at right; 3-line\nimprint at left\\] THE | MODERN LIBRARY | NEW YORK | \\[rule\\]\n\nPagination and collation as 239a.\n\nContents as 239a except: \\[iv\\] A HIGH WIND IN JAMAICA | (THE INNOCENT\nVOYAGE) | COPYRIGHT, 1929, BY RICHARD HUGHES | INTRODUCTION COPYRIGHT,\n1932, | BY THE MODERN LIBRARY, INC.\n\n> *Variant:* Pagination as 239a except: \\[400–410\\].\n> \\[1–13\\]16 \\[14\\]12. Front flap as 239a. (*Fall 1942*)"

\n> The perpetual enchantment and all the magical and multi-colored\n> pageantry of *The Arabian Nights* are offered here in Sir Richard\n> Burton’s rendering for the delectation of adult minds. Shahrazad spins\n> her tales without censorial interruption, and the spell that possessed\n> King Shahryar and since then has possessed mankind is not broken,\n> praise be to Allah, by discreet asterisks and decorous euphemisms. The\n> tales in this volume were chosen because they are the most fascinating\n> and representative of *The Book of a Thousand Nights and a Night*.\n> (*Fall 1937*)\n\nOriginal ML selection. Published September 1932. *WR* 15 October 1932.\nFirst printing: Not ascertained.\n\nThe title page of *The Arabian Nights’ Entertainments* had so much text\nthat there was no room for the torchbearer or for Cerf and Klopfer’s\nnames in the imprint.\n\nCerf selected the stories included in the ML edition. The statement on\nthe title page that the stories were “complete and unabridged” was not\nstrictly true. Cerf explained his editorial approach and interpretation\nof “unabridged” as follows: “As far as the stories being complete and\nunabridged is concerned, the only thing that was left out of any of the\nstories included was long stretches of poetry, and a very occasional\nphrase that was of no importance whatever to the story. In every sense I\nbelieve it thoroughly fair to say that the stories are quite unabridged”\n(Cerf to Herman Schaff, John W. Luce & Co., 16 November 1932). The ML\nmade three sets of plates, two of which were sold for $950 each to\nWalter J. Black and Blue Ribbon Books. Black had first rights to the\nbook for mail-order purposes. The ML followed with its edition in\nSeptember 1932, and Blue Ribbon Books brought out an illustrated edition\nin November (Cerf to Walter J. Black, 13 January 1932). Grosset & Dunlap\nborrowed the ML’s plates in 1937 to print a motion picture edition,\npaying the ML a $1,000 advance against royalties of 10 cents a copy\n(Cerf to Edward Edelson, Grosset & Dunlap, 28 May 1937).\n\n*The Arabian Nights* sold 7,578 copies during the eighteen-month period\nMay 1942–October 1943, placing it low in the first quarter of ML and\nGiant titles. It did not rank among the 100 best-selling ML titles in\nthe regular ML during the twelve-month period November 1951–October\n1952.\n\n#### 244b. (*Fall 1932*)\n\n> Front flap:
\n> *Yama the Pit* is as sinister and pitiless as the vice it exposes and\n> condemns. It omits nothing and condones nothing of the abominations of\n> the oldest profession in the world. The stark picture of a brothel and\n> the prostitute’s way of life becomes by the artistry of Alexandre\n> Kuprin an indictment more scathing and effective than all the moral\n> indignation of vice crusaders and the periodical revelations of the\n> police. *Yama* carries horror to a point beyond horror and into the\n> realm of great realistic literature. ML\nedition printed from plates made from a new typesetting based on the\n1929 edition. Publication scheduled for October 1932 but moved forward\nto September when Marx’s *Capital, The Communist Manifesto and Other\nWritings* (246) was postponed. *WR* 15 October 1932. First printing: Not\nascertained. Discontinued fall 1952.\n\nGuerney’s introduction to the 1929 edition was revised for the ML.\n\nThe ML paid Guerney a $2,000 advance against royalties of 10 cents a\ncopy. The contract specified that royalties were to drop to 9½ cents\nafter the first 20,000 copies. Cerf originally offered Guerney royalties\nof 9½ cents on all copies but increased the offer following negotiations\nwith Guerney. The $800 cost of making new plates was split between\nGuerney and the ML. Guerney’s half was to be charged against royalties\nafter sales passed 20,000 copies, and the plates were to remain the\nproperty of the ML (Cerf to Guerney, 17 February 1931).\n\n*Yama* sold 2,700 copies during the eighteen-month period May\n1942–October 1943, placing it in the fourth quarter of ML and Giant\ntitles.\n\n#### 245b. First printing (1932)\n\n\\[within double rules\\] CAPITAL | THE COMMUNIST MANIFESTO | AND OTHER\nWRITINGS | \\[rule\\] | BY | KARL MARX | \\[rule\\] | EDITED, WITH AN\nINTRODUCTION, BY | MAX EASTMAN | \\[rule\\] | WITH AN UNPUBLISHED ESSAY ON\nMARXISM | BY | LENIN | \\[rule\\] | \\[torchbearer A2\\] | \\[rule\\] |\nBENNETT A. CERF · DONALD S. Contents as 246a except:\n> \\[431–435\\] ML list; \\[436–437\\] ML Giants list; \\[438\\] blank. (*Fall\n> 1939*)\n\n*Contents:* Part I. Outlines of a Future\nSociety. *From* The German Ideology, translated by Max Eastman –\n*From* The Poverty of Philosophy – *From* The Criticism of the Gotha\nProgram. Part II. Analysis of the\nMaterials. *From* The German Ideology, translated by Max Eastman\n– *From* the introduction to Critique of Political Economy, translated\nby Max Eastman – Capital (selections), translated by Stephen L. Trask –\nThe Theory of Crises, by Julian Borchardt. Part\nIII. The Method and the Call to Action. The Communist Manifesto –\n*From* Criticism of the Gotha Program – Address of the Central Authority\nto the Communist League, translated by Max Eastman – The Civil War in\nFrance.\n\n*Jacket* *A:* Uniform philosophy jacket in deep red (13), dark gray\n(266) and black on cream paper; borders in deep red, lettering in black.\nJacket title on front panel of jacket A and all subsequent jackets:\nCAPITAL AND OTHER WRITINGS. Signed: WC. (*Fall 1932*)\n\n*Jacket B:* Pictorial in deep reddish orange (36) and black on cream\npaper depicting Marx with marching workers in background; borders in\ndeep reddish orange, lettering in black.\n\n> Front flap:
\n> With one-sixth of the habitable world governed by Marxian doctrines\n> and with the rest of the world facing the possibility of a Communist\n> social order, the writings of Karl Marx become more a modern\n> revelation and prophecy than a mere economic interpretation of\n> history. His *Capital and Other Writings*, judiciously and\n> discriminatingly edited by Max Eastman, is the contemporary Bible of a\n> newly emerged society, and as such it has an eminent place on the\n> shelf of the Modern Library. (*Fall 1935*)\n\nOriginal ML collection. The abridgment of the three volumes of *Capital*\nand Borchardt’s essay, “The Theory of Crises” (pp. 13–314) are a reprint\nof *The People’s Marx: Abridged Popular Edition of the Three Volumes of\n“Capital,”* edited by Julian Borchardt and translated by Stephen L.\nTrask (London: International Bookshops, 1921). Publication of the ML\nedition originally scheduled for September 1932 but postponed until\nOctober because of additions to the manuscript. *WR* 12 November 1932.\nFirst printing: Not ascertained.\n\n*The People’s Marx* does not appear to have been copyrighted in the U.S.\nThe ML reprinted it with the permission of the translator, Stephen L.\nTrask. Eastman notes, “This book itself has almost become a classic, and\nit seems to me a better way to get acquainted with Marx’s economic\ntheories than merely to read through the first volume and stop, as is\nusually done” (p. xix). The selections from *The German Ideology* and\n*Critique of Political Economy* in Part II were added by the ML.\n\nCerf was responsible for the inclusion of the essay by Lenin which was\nprominently noted on the title page and dust jacket. At the time he was\ncompiling the volume Eastman referred to it as “that rather heavy\nencyclopedia-article of Lenin’s” and wanted to omit it so that more of\nMarx’s own writings could be included (Eastman to Cerf, 6 May 1932).\nCerf responded, “I do want to leave in the Lenin article . . . since his\nname may add materially to the sale of the volume.” He indicated that he\nwould cut it only if Eastman insisted (Cerf to Eastman, 9 May 1932). The\nLenin essay that was included, “The Three Sources and Three Constituent\nParts of Marxism,” may have been different from the one originally\nprojected. Eastman states in the volume: “I have translated an\nintroduction to Marxism written by Lenin in 1913 and published in a\nRussian magazine called *Education*. To this very orthodox statement by\nLenin it might be well—in view of my own proposals—to add another remark\nmade by him: ‘In no sense do we regard the Marxist theory as something\ncomplete and unassailable. On the contrary, we are convinced that this\ntheory is only the corner-stone of that science which socialists must\nadvance in all directions if they do not wish to fall behind life” (p.\nxix).\n\nShortly after the publication of *Capital, The Communist Manifesto, and\nOther Writings*, Klopfer invited Eastman to translate a volume of\nLenin’s writings for the ML. Eastman signed a contract to translate and\nedit the book, but other work got in the way and the contract was\ncancelled eighteen months later. See “Titles Sought, Suggested,\nDeclined” under 1933 for more information.\n\nM. Lincoln Schuster of Simon and Schuster wrote to Cerf about the lack\nof an index to *Capital, The Communist Manifesto, and Other Writings*:\n“I think it is an outrage to publish DAS KAPITAL by Karl Marx without an\nindex. On what page, for example, is the letter of instruction which\nKarl Marx wrote from London in 1850 to the Communist League? Remember\nyour obligation to scholarship, Bennett: you’re publishing Karl not\nGroucho” (Schuster to Cerf, 7 February 1933). Klopfer replied that\nSchuster should blame Eastman, who never suggested an index (Klopfer to\nSchuster, 8 February 1933).\n\nSales figures from the 1930s are not available. *Capital, The Communist\nManifesto and Other Writings* sold 4,391 copies during the\neighteen-month period May 1942–October 1943, placing it in the third\nquarter of ML and Giant titles. It rose to the first quarter of ML and\nGiant titles during the twelve-month period November 1951–October 1952\nwith sales of 4,530 copies. In 1954 Eastman noted that *Capital, The\nCommunist Manifesto and Other Writings* had sold nearly 4,000 copies in\nthe past six months (Eastman to Cerf, 19 August 1954).\n\nThe ML published an unabridged edition of the first volume of Marx’s\n*Capital* (G24) in the Giants series in 1936. The Giant sold 8,854\ncopies during the eighteen-month period May 1942–October 1943 and 4,333\ncopies during the twelve-month period November 1951–October 1952,\nplacing it in the first quarter of ML and Giant titles at both periods.\n\n#### 246b. First printing (1932)\n\n\\[within double rules\\] THREE SOLDIERS | \\[rule\\] | BY | JOHN DOS PASSOS\n| \\[rule\\] | INTRODUCTION BY | JOHN DOS PASSOS | \\[rule\\] |\n\\[torchbearer C1\\] | \\[rule\\] | BENNETT A. CERF : DONALD S. KLOPFER |\nTHE MODERN LIBRARY | PUBLISHERS\n\nPp. \\[i–iv\\] v–ix \\[x–xii\\], \\[1–2\\] 3–471 \\[472\\].\n\\[1–14\\]16 \\[15\\]16(16+1.2)\n\n\\[i\\] half title; \\[ii\\] pub. note D12; \\[iii\\] title; \\[iv\\]\n*Copyright*, 1921, *by* GEORGE H. DORAN COMPANY | \\[short double rule\\]\n| *Intro. copyright*, 1932, *by* THE MODERN LIBRARY, INC. | \\[short\ndouble rule\\] | *First Modern Library Edition* | 1932; v–ix INTRODUCTION\nsigned p. ix: John Dos Passos. |\nProvincetown, | June, 1932.; \\[x\\] blank; \\[xi\\] CONTENTS; \\[xii\\]\nblank; \\[1\\] part title: Part One: MAKING\nTHE MOULD; \\[2\\] epigraph from Stendhal; 3–471 text; \\[472\\] blank.\n*Note:* Pp. 469– \\[472\\] are an inserted fold.\n\n> *Variant:* Pp. \\[i–iv\\] v–ix \\[x–xii\\], \\[1–2\\] 3–471 \\[472–476\\].\n> \\[1–15\\]16 \\[16\\]4. Contents as 248a except:\n> \\[iv\\] *First* statement omitted; \\[472–476\\] blank.\n\n*Jacket:* Pictorial in brownish orange (54) and black on pale orange\nyellow (73) paper depicting three soldiers learning against a ship’s\nrail with black sea in background; borders in brownish orange, lettering\nin black. Signed: W.C. (*Fall 1932*)\n\n> Front flap:
\n> The years that have passed since the World War have been severe to the\n> literature of that disastrous episode in human history. Only a few\n> books have survived. Of these, *Three Soldiers* stands pre-eminent. It\n> reveals the cant and manufactured romanticism of war. Through the\n> experiences of three men of the ranks, humanity’s most ghastly venture\n> in mutual destruction stands condemned as criminal and futile. *Three\n> Soldiers* introduced a writer who has since become a major figure\n> among American novelists. Upon this first book his reputation rests\n> securely. (*Spring 1935*)\n\nOriginally published by George H. Doran Co., 1921. ML edition printed\nfrom Doubleday, Doran plates made from a new typesetting; the new plates\nappear to have been used exclusively by the ML. Publication scheduled\nfor November 1932. *WR* 17 December 1932. First printing: 6,000 copies.\nDiscontinued 1963.\n\nEight months before the ML edition was published Cerf wrote Doubleday,\nDoran, “We think that we ought to have a Dos Passos book in the Modern\nLibrary, and we can’t make up our minds between the 42nd Parallel and\nThree Soldiers.” He asked how many copies *Three Soldiers* had sold in\nthe trade edition and the subsequent reprint in Doubleday’s Sun Dial\nLibrary (Cerf to Robert de Graff, 9 April 1932). When he discovered that\nthe plates for *Three Soldiers* were too large for the ML’s format he\ncontacted Harper & Brothers about the comparative sales of Dos Passos’s\n*Manhattan Transfer* and *The 42nd Parallel*. *Manhattan Transfer* had\nsold 25,000 copies in contrast to 12,000 for *The 42nd Parallel* (Henry\nHoyns, Harper & Bros., to Cerf, 12 April 1932), but the plates of those\ntitles were also too large for the ML. In the end Cerf decided to begin\nwith *Three Soldiers* and added *The 42nd Parallel* (307) to the ML five\nyears later. Since new typesettings were required for both titles, Dos\nPassos was able to revise the texts for the ML editions.\n\nFor *Three Soldiers* the ML paid Doubleday, Doran an advance of $1,250\nagainst royalties of 10 cents a copy. When Dos Passos submitted his\ncorrections and revisions for the ML edition he told Cerf, “Let me know\nif I made more corrections in the *Three Soldiers* text than you can\nhandle—I didn’t know if it was going to be reset or if the plates were\njust going to be photographed down. The text is in awful shape as I was\naway when it was published and didn’t correct any proofs.” He also asked\nCerf to limit changes in his introduction to the ML edition to spelling\ncorrections and to leave word arrangement and punctuation as they were.\nThe introduction, he noted, “turned out a very long job, but while I was\ndoing it I thought I’d better give ’em the works” (Dos Passos to Cerf,\nundated).\n\n*Three Soldiers* sold 4,102 copies during the eighteen-month period May\n1942–October 1943, placing it in the third quarter of ML and Giant\ntitles. When Houghton Mifflin acquired the publishing rights from\nDoubleday in 1946, Klopfer noted that sales had averaged about 2,500\ncopies a year (Klopfer to Lovell Thompson, Houghton Mifflin Co., 20\nAugust 1946). *Three Soldiers* did not rank among the 100 best-selling\ntitles in the regular ML during the twelve-month period November\n1951–October 1952.\n\nHoughton Mifflin withdrew *Three Soldiers* and Dos Passos’s *U.S.A.*\n(G42) from the ML in June 1961 when the firm launched its quality\npaperback series, Sentry Editions. Klopfer signed the termination\nagreement in June and instructed the production department to make no\nmore printings of either title (Klopfer to “Gorgeous Redhead,” 14 June\n1961). Houghton Mifflin allowed the ML to sell off its existing stock\nand asked how long it would take, explaining that they did not want to\nbring out *Three Soldiers* until the ML stock was exhausted (Hardwick\nMoseley, Houghton Mifflin, to Klopfer, 21 June 1961). Klopfer indicated\nthat the ML had 3,000 copies of *Three Soldiers*, enough to last about a\nyear, and a six- to eight-month supply of *U.S.A.* (Klopfer to Moseley,\n22 June 1961). Moseley wrote at the beginning of 1964 to inquire if\n*Three Soldiers* was out of stock, and Klopfer replied that it was\n(Moseley to Klopfer, 15 January 1964; Klopfer to Moseley, 22 January\n1964). *Three Soldiers* was published in Sentry Editions later that\nyear.\n\n*Three Soldiers* was dropped from separately published ML catalogs after\nfall 1963, but it continued to be included in lists of ML titles at the\nend of ML volumes through 1970. Its ML number was reassigned to *The\nBuddhist Tradition*, edited by Wm. Theodore de Bary (613), in spring\n1969 after the oversight was discovered.\n\n#### 248b.       "full": "***DANTE ALIGHIERI. THE DIVINE COMEDY. 1932–1971. (ML 208)**\n\n#### 251.1a. First printing (1932)\n\n\\[within double rules\\] THE DIVINE COMEDY | \\[rule\\] | OF | DANTE\nALIGHIERI | \\[rule\\] | THE CARLYLE-WICKSTEED | TRANSLATION | \\[rule\\] |\nINTRODUCTION BY | PROFESSOR C. H. GRANDGENT | OF HARVARD UNIVERSITY |\n\\[rule\\] | \\[torchbearer A2\\] | \\[rule\\] | BENNETT A. CERF · DONALD S.\nKLOPFER | THE MODERN LIBRARY | NEW YORK\n\nPp. \\[i–iv\\] v–xiii \\[xiv–xvi\\], \\[1–2\\] 3–601 \\[602–608\\].\n\\[1–19\\]16 \\[20\\]8\n\n\\[i\\] half title; \\[ii\\] pub. note A6; \\[iii\\] title; \\[iv\\]\n*Copyright,* 1932, *by* THE MODERN LIBRARY, INC. | \\[short double rule\\]\n| *First Modern Library Edition* | 1932; v–xiii INTRODUCTION signed p.\nxiii: C. H. Grandgent. | Cambridge, Mass. | *September,* 1932.; \\[xiv\\]\nblank; \\[xv\\] CONTENTS; \\[xvi\\] blank; \\[1\\] fly title; \\[2\\] blank; 3–5\nPUBLISHER’S NOTE; \\[6\\] blank; \\[7\\] part title: INFERNO; \\[8\\] blank;\n9–11 NOTE ON DANTE’S HELL; 12–14 THE CHRONOLOGY OF THE “INFERNO”; 15–16\ndiagrams and description of concentric spheres and eight revolving\nheavens; \\[17\\] epigraph in Latin from Seneca; \\[18\\] blank; 19–601\ntext; \\[602\\] epigraph from Bernard; \\[603–607\\] ML list; \\[608\\] blank.\n(*Fall 1932*) *Note:* *First* statement retained on printings through\nfall 1935.\n\n*Jacket A:* Pictorial in deep yellowish pink (27) and black on cream\npaper depicting Dante with the inferno, purgatory, and paradise in\nbackground represented by flames, souls making their way up a mountain,\nand sunshine and clouds; borders in deep yellowish pink, lettering in\nblack. Signed: L. (*Fall 1932*)\n\n> Front flap:
\n> How to make available to the contemporary reader all the beauty of\n> thought, language and imagery of *The Divine Comedy* has been the\n> primary consideration of the editors of the Modern Library in issuing\n> this translated version of Dante’s masterpiece, complete and\n> unabridged. After careful consideration of all the extant English\n> translations and after consultation with the foremost university\n> authorities, the Carlyle-Wicksteed rendering was chosen. It gives the\n> entire scope, substance and spirit of *The Divine Comedy* to those\n> numberless readers who cannot cope with the original. (*Fall 1935*)\n\n*Jacket B:* Pictorial in vivid red (11) and strong brown (55) on coated\ncream paper depicting Dante with an open book standing on a hillside\noverlooking the walls of a city, viewed through a border of stone\nportals; background within borders in vivid red with title and statement\nof responsibility for translation in reverse, other lettering in strong\nbrown. Designed by Paul Galdone, November 1937; unsigned. Front flap as\njacket A. (*Fall 1937*)\n\nCarlyle-Okey-Wicksteed translation originally published in England by\nJ. M. Dent & Sons (Temple Classics, 3 vols., around 1900). ML edition\nprinted from plates made from a new typesetting. Published December\n1932. *WR* 31 December 1932. First printing: Not ascertained.\nDiscontinued 1971/72. Also published in ML Paperbacks, 1955, and\nsubsequently in Vintage Books.\n\nThe ML selected the Carlyle-Okey-Wicksteed translation on the basis of\nreports from faculty members at Yale and Columbia and information from\nHenry C. Moriarty of the Harvard Cooperative Bookstore that it was the\ntranslation favored at Harvard. Cerf also sought the advice of Maurice\nFiruski of the Housatonuc Bookshop in Salisbury, Connecticut, about the\ntranslation, introduction, and notes. Cerf wanted an introduction by\nT. S. Eliot and offered Faber & Faber $60 to reprint Eliot’s essay on\nDante. When that offer was rejected Firuski recommended C. H. Grandgent\nof Harvard University. Cerf still hoped to get Eliot (Cerf to Firuski,\n28 June 1932) but wrote Grandgent a month later offering $75 for an\nintroduction, which he indicated was needed by mid-September (Cerf to\nGrandgent, 25 July 1932). Grandgent sent the introduction in August.\n\nCerf was pleased with the introduction but asked Grandgent to add a few\nparagraphs about the translation (Cerf to Grandgent, 18 August 1932).\nGrandgent replied that he had not read the translation the ML was using\nand knew nothing of the circumstances surrounding it. He thought the\nbest translation he had read was that of Jefferson B. Fletcher\n(Macmillan, 1931) and indicated that he wouldn’t want to give it an\ninferior position (Grandgent to Cerf, 19 August 1932). Cerf sent him the\nTemple Classics edition, and Grandgent supplied an additional paragraph\nby the end of August. He wrote: “After careful consideration of all of\nthe English translations of Dante, the work of John Aiken Carlyle,\nThomas Okey, and P. H. Wicksteed was chosen. It is a translation that is\nclear, dignified, and accurate, in simple, idiomatic prose. It can be\nreadily followed without any reference to the original Italian text. Its\nscholarly notes cover all obscure points more than adequately” (p.\nxiii).\n\nThe Publisher’s Note states: “For this Modern Library edition of Dante’s\n*Divine Comedy* the best translations have been followed: *Inferno*, by\nJohn Aiken Carlyle; *Purgatorio*, by Thomas Okey; *Paradisio*, by Philip\nH. Wicksteed. The notes (edited for this edition by Julie Eidesheim)\nfollow, in the main, the excellent notes for the *Inferno* and the\n*Purgatorio* by Dr. H. Oeslsner, and those prepared jointly by Dr.\nOeslner and Philip H. Wicksteed for the *Paradisio*” (251.1, p. 3;\n251.2, p. xvii).\n\nML title pages and jackets from 1932 through the early 1960s referred to\nthe translation as the “Carlyle-Wicksteed translation,” overlooking the\ntranslator of *Purgatorio*. Printings in the Illustrated Modern Library\n(IML 7) also omitted Okey’s name from the title page. The translations\nwere not fully identified on the title page or jackets of regular ML\nprintings until 1963/64 (251.2c).\n\nShortly after Jason Epstein joined RH in 1958 he invited Charles S.\nSingleton of Johns Hopkins University to prepare a new translation of\n*The Divine Comedy* for the ML. Singleton indicated that he was working\non a prose translation for the Bollingen Foundation, which was scheduled\nto be published by Princeton University Press in 1965, the\nseven-hundredth anniversary of Dante’s birth. Epstein secured the\napproval of the Bollingen Foundation for a ML edition to appear after\ntheirs, and a contract was signed with Singleton, who received advances\ntotaling three thousand dollars between 1959 and 1961. The project took\nlonger than anticipated, however. It was not ready by 1965. Two years\nlater it was still being listed as a future title in ML memoranda. The\nBollingen Foundation edition appeared in six volumes between 1970 and\n1975 with the text in Italian and English and with extensive commentary.\nBy that time it was too late for a ML edition. The ML had become\nmoribund and had ceased adding new titles.\n\n*The Divine Comedy* sold 8,362 copies during the eighteen-month period\nMay 1942–October 1943, placing it in the first quarter ML and Giant\ntitles. It sold 7,590 copies during the twelve-month period November\n1951–October 1952, making it fifteenth out of the 100 best-selling\ntitles in the regular ML. Sales totaled 151,638 copies by spring 1958.\n\n#### 251.1b. Title page reset (1941)\n\n*The Divine Comedy* | *of |* DANTE ALIGHIERI | *The* CARLYLE-WICKSTEED\n*Translation* | *Introduction by* | PROFESSOR C. H.   "id": "1932",
  "searchContent": " MODERN LIBRARY SERIES 1932 1932 Elmer Adler, who had been a director of Random House since the imprint\nwas created in 1927 to distribute and publish fine limited editions,\nresigned in 1932. Thereafter he devoted himself exclusively to his fine\nprinting business, Pynson Printers, and to *The Colophon*, the\nbibliophilic quarterly which he published from 1930 to 1940. Random\nHouse was reorganized—still as a subsidiary of The Modern Library,\nInc.—with Cerf and Klopfer as sole directors. It was not until 1933,\nfollowing the bankruptcy of Liveright, Inc., that Cerf and Klopfer\nturned seriously to trade publishing.\n\nML Giants, which had been introduced in fall 1931, continued to provide\nexceptional value in the Depression book market. The four volumes\npublished in 1932 sold for $1.00 each and averaged more than 1,300 pages\nper volume.\n\nThe ML published a full-page ad in *Publishers’ Weekly* that lampooned\nthe publishing industry adage, “You can’t sell books like soap.”\nAdapting the widely known slogan of Ivory Soap, the ad is headed “They\nFloat!” and depicts an attractive young woman seated in her bath with\nfive Modern Library books—*Of Human Bondage*, *Swann’s Way*, *The Magic\nMountain*, *Droll Stories*, and *Sanctuary*—floating on the surface of\nthe water (*PW*, 14 May 1932, p. 2015). Twenty-four titles were added and fifteen were discontinued, bringing\nthe number of titles in the regular ML to 207. Six of the discontinued\ntitles (two by Maupassant and four by Wilde) were repackaged as three\nvolumes to offer better value. Chekhov, *Rothschild’s Fiddle and Other\nStories*, which had been in the ML since 1917, was replaced by a more\ncomprehensive collection, *The Stories of Anton Tchekov*, edited by\nRobert Linscott). Three new titles in ML Giants, including Gibbon,\n*Decline and Fall of the Roman Empire* in two volumes, brought the\nnumber of Giants to six titles in seven volumes. *Decline and Fall of\nthe Roman Empire* was expanded to three volumes in 1946. All new titles except Dreiser, *Sister Carrie* (230) were published in\nthe standard format with the binding measuring 6⅝ x 4⅜ in. (168 x 110\nmm) and the leaves trimmed to 6½ x 4¼ in. (164 x 107 mm). *Sister\nCarrie* was ¼ in. taller.\n\nThe ML’s standard format was enlarged in 1939. The new binding measured\n7¼ x 4⅞ in. (182 x 123 mm) with a trim size of 7 x 4¾ in. (177 x 120\nmm). In 1969 a taller, slightly slimmer format was introduced with the\nbinding measuring 7½ x 4¾ in. (190 x 120 mm) and a trim size of 7¼ x 4½\nin. (182 x 115 mm). All dimensions indicated are approximate.\n\nMost books through 1954 were printed with 16 pages on each side of the\nsheet and bound in gatherings of 16 leaves (32 pages); by 1956 most\nbooks were being printed with 32 pages on each side of the sheet and\nbound in gatherings of 32 leaves (64 pages). All new titles had the final version of Elmer Adler’s title page with\nthe title in open-face type; all but three had torchbearer A2.\nDostoyevsky, *Crime and Punishment* (228) had torchbearer A3; Dos\nPassos, *Three Soldiers* (248) had torchbearer C1; *The Arabian Nights’\nEntertainments* (244) had no torchbearer on the title page. All new\ntitles except *The Arabian Nights’ Entertainments* had the 3-line\nimprint that began to be used in January 1931:\n\nBENNETT A. CERF · DONALD S. KLOPFER\n\nT H E M O D E R N L I B R A R Y\n\nNEW YORK\n\nThe title page of *The Arabian Nights’ Entertainments* had so much text\nthat there was no room for a torchbearer or Cerf and Klopfer’s names.\n\nThe imprint for newly published titles reverted to the previous 2-line\nformat in March 1936 after the Modern Library acquired the publishing\nfirm Harrison Smith and Robert Haas, and Haas and Smith joined Cerf and\nKlopfer as partners. Balloon cloth binding D, with Kent’s torchbearer (1⅛ in.; 27 mm) stamped\nin gold on the front panel and stylized initials “ML” added in gold\nabove the 2-line imprint on the spine.\n\nBalloon cloth bindings continued to be available in four colors: red,\nblue, green, and brown, with each title published simultaneously in all\nfour bindings. Rockwell Kent endpapers in moderate orange (53). Moderate orange\nendpapers were used with all balloon cloth bindings from fall 1930\nthrough spring 1939, except for three spring 1939 titles that were\npublished in the larger format that the ML would adopt for all titles\nbeginning that fall. An enlarged version of Kent’s endpaper was\nintroduced in spring 1940. The central panels featuring Kent’s\ntorchbearer were unchanged, but the surrounding patterns of open books\nand “ml” initials were extended to fill the larger space.\n\n**Jackets**\n\nEighteen of the 1932 titles were published in individually designed\npictorial jackets. Four were published in uniform typographic jacket D\nand two in uniform typographic jacket F. Nearly all new titles published\nbetween 1932 and the introduction of the ML’s larger format in fall 1939\nhad pictorial jackets. Uniform typographic jacket D was used on newly\npublished titles for the last time in spring 1932. The final examples of\nits use, *Poems of Longfellow* (235) and Franklin, *Autobiography and\nSelections from His Other Writings* (236), provoked an angry letter from\nCerf, who had apparently given instructions that the jacket was no\nlonger to be used for new titles. 95 cents. (Spring) Dreiser, *Sister Carrie* xDickens, *Pickwick Papers*; Giants\nthrough G5. (Fall) Dickens, *Pickwick Papers* xLewis, *Arrowsmith*;\nGiants through G7. Cerf approached Arthur Pell at Liveright, Inc., about including a volume\nof plays by Eugene O’Neill in ML Giants (Cerf to Pell, 16 November\n1932). Nothing came of this initiative. O’Neill became a Random House\nauthor the following year after the Liveright bankruptcy. O’Neill’s\n*Nine Plays* (G53), originally published by Liveright in 1932, was added\nto the Giants in 1941.\n\nAfter repeated attempts to get reprint rights to James Stephens’s *Crock\nof Gold* (Macmillan, 1912) Cerf enlisted the assistance of Hugh Eayrs,\nthe president of the Macmillan Co. of Canada. Eayrs wrote to the\npresident of the Macmillan Co. in New York urging him to allow a ML\nreprint (Eayrs to George Brett, Jr., 16 May 1932). Brett replied, “We\nare simply not going to give it to him . . . if we should want to do a\ncheap edition we would do it ourselves” (Brett to Eayrs, 24 May 1932).\n\nCerf contacted Hermann Schaff for the rights to an edition of Synge\nplays, saying “If we can’t get this from you by peaceable means in the\nnext two years, I will have to start negotiations with the Boston\nunderworld to spirit you off somewhere and tickle your bare soles with\nlighted matches until you sign on the dotted line” (Cerf to Schaff, 16\nNovember 1932). Cerf again contacted Doubleday for reprint rights to\n*Kim* (Cerf to Nelson Doubleday, 3 December 1932). Cerf was beginning to\nconsider an anthology of Yiddish literature as early as January 1932.\n\nJames Crowder, the ML’s sales representative for the Middle West,\nsuggested Edmund Wilson’s *Axel’s Castle: A Study in the Imaginative\nLiterature of 1870–1930*, which had been published by Charles Scribner’s\nSons in 1931 (Crowder to Cerf, 4 June 1932). Cerf corresponded with\nWilson about a ML edition in 1933. Wilson was interested and suggested\nadding several additional essays to the ML edition, which he proposed\npublishing under a new title such as *Literary Essays* or *Essays on\nModern Authors*. He also asked for an advance of $500 (Wilson to Cerf,\n28 September 1933). In the end Wilson decided to incorporate the essays\nhe had considered adding to *Axel’s Castle* into a new book (Wilson to\nCerf, 6 November 1933).\n\nDale Warren, the head of publicity at Houghton Mifflin Co., suggested a\none-volume condensation of Proust, noting confidentially that the firm\nof Albert & Charles Boni, which then published the seven volumes\ncomprising Proust’s *Remembrance of Things Past*, would welcome some\nready cash (Warren to Cerf, 20 June 1932). Charles Boni had left in\n1930, and the firm “was in major trouble” by the early 1930s (“Albert\nand Charles Boni” in *American Literary Publishing Houses, 1900–1980*,\np. 57).\n\nThe ML had already reprinted the first two volumes, *Swann’s Way* (1928)\nand *Within a Budding Grove* (1930), and would add *Guermantes Way* in\n1933. Cerf thought that Warren’s suggestion was an excellent idea except\nfrom the point of view of the publisher of the complete set. He asked\nWarren to keep the idea under his hat and not even tell Ferris\nGreenslet, the manager of the Houghton Mifflin trade department. “We\nhave had two conversations with Boni relative to buying the entire\nProust property from him. In the event of a slow down, I think we might\nbe able to get these books for our Random House list. In the event that\nanything happens along this line, I will let you know as promptly as\npossible. Something must break in this situation before the\nsummer is very much advanced (Cerf to Warren, 21 June 1932; underlining\nin original). Five months later he indicated, “I have not given up hopes\nof buying the entire Proust property from Albert Boni. If anything\ndevelops along this line, be assured that I will get in touch with you\nimmediately” (Cerf to Warren, 22 November 1932). Cerf was able to secure\n*Remembrance of Things Past* a year or so later. The Random House\nedition was published as a four-volume set in a wooden slipcase; Cerf\ndescribed it as “one of the typographical masterpieces of 1934” (Cerf,\n*At Random*, p. 99).\n\nWarren never gave up his proposal for a one-volume condensation of\nProust. When he repeated the idea in 1943, Cerf turned him down but\nadded that he would give Houghton Mifflin rights to a condensed Proust\nif the firm would allow the ML to reprint Willa Cather’s *My Antonia*,\nwhich he had been trying to get for the series since 1925 (Cerf to\nWarren, 13 September 1943). Dostoyevsky, *Crime and Punishment* (1932) 228\n\nMann, *Magic Mountain* (1932) 229\n\nDreiser, *Sister Carrie* (1932) 230\n\nStoker, *Dracula* (1932) 231\n\nChekhov, *Stories of Anton Tchekov* (1932–1956), *Stories of Anton\nChekhov* (1957–1963), *Short Stories of Anton Chekhov* (1964– ) 232\n\nFaulkner, *Sanctuary* (1932) 233\n\nHardy, *Tess of the D’Urbervilles* (1932) 234\n\nLongfellow, *Poems* (1932–1945), *Poems of Henry Wadsworth Longfellow*\n(1945– ) 235\n\nFranklin, *Autobiography Benjamin Franklin and Selections from His Other\nWritings* (1932) 236\n\nHemingway, *Farewell to Arms* (1932) 237\n\nConrad, *Victory* (1932) 238\n\nHughes, *High Wind in Jamaica* (1932) 239\n\n*Eight Famous Elizabethan Plays* (1932) 240\n\n***  \nSpring*** Elmer Adler, who had been a director of Random House since the imprint\nwas created in 1927 to distribute and publish fine limited editions,\nresigned in 1932. Thereafter he devoted himself exclusively to his fine\nprinting business, Pynson Printers, and to *The Colophon*, the\nbibliophilic quarterly which he published from 1930 to 1940. Random\nHouse was reorganized—still as a subsidiary of The Modern Library,\nInc.—with Cerf and Klopfer as sole directors. It was not until 1933,\nfollowing the bankruptcy of Liveright, Inc., that Cerf and Klopfer\nturned seriously to trade publishing. [object Object]"
